This is a packaging <em>decision</em>, not a feature, and it does not advance any open 1.0 acceptance criterion. Shipping 1.0 stays on the working embeddable-Python bundle (<a href="../../scripts/build_windows_distribution.py">scripts/build_windows_distribution.py</a>), which also keeps the v1.1 plugin path trivial. The section below is the full analysis and the go/no-go gate for revisiting freezing in 2.0.</p> </blockquote> <p><strong>The starting point (honest).</strong> QUILL today ships raw <code>quill/**/*.py</code> source alongside a pinned official <strong>python-3.12.6-embed-amd64</strong> runtime. The source on disk is fully readable and editable by any end user. The PRD's <em>intended</em> frozen build (§10.6) names <strong>PyInstaller one-folder</strong> but is unimplemented. So the real choice is three-way: (a) today's embeddable-Python + source, (b) PyInstaller one-folder, (c) <strong>free Nuitka <code>--standalone</code></strong>. <a href="https://nuitka.net/">Nuitka</a> is a Python→C optimizing compiler.</p> <p><strong>Framing assumptions that shape the verdict.</strong></p> <ul> <li><strong>Free Nuitka only.</strong> QUILL is MIT-licensed in a public repo. Nuitka Commercial's headline — obfuscate source/constants/keys against reverse engineering (€250–400/yr) — is <strong>structurally moot</strong> because the source is already public. Paying to hide a public codebase is incoherent, so the commercial tier is disqualified outright and every gain below is from <em>free</em> Nuitka. That deletes Nuitka's marquee feature from the benefit column.</li> <li><strong>Standalone (one-folder), never onefile.</strong> Onefile self-extracts to temp on every launch (slower cold start, AV/SmartScreen false positives). The PRD already chose folder mode for exactly this reason; the same constraint applies.</li> </ul> <p><strong>Real gains (free Nuitka).</strong></p> <ul> <li><strong>G1 — Startup/import speed (the only measurable technical gain).</strong> Compiled module bodies skip parse + bytecode-compile, so cold-start import is genuinely faster than both alternatives. Bounded and modest: the win is in import/startup, <strong>not</strong> steady-state editing — once the UI is up you are in wxPython event loops and native DLLs (TTS, llama, OCR) that Nuitka cannot speed up. It is a lever for PERF-9's startup budget, but deferred imports in pure source can also hit that budget, so Nuitka is <em>a</em> lever, not the only one.</li> <li><strong>G2 — A real binary you authored (the genuine non-perf gain).</strong> Today QUILL signs and ships someone else's <code>python.exe</code>. Nuitka produces a real PE you compiled: cleaner <code>signtool</code> identity, faster SmartScreen reputation accrual, and tamper resistance against casual in-place edits of the writing-path source. This is operational hygiene and integrity, <strong>not</strong> IP protection (free Nuitka leaves constants/data readable, and the source is on GitHub anyway).</li> <li><strong>G3 — Marginally tighter layout.</strong> Easily erased by the native dependency payload (llama/OCR/dictionaries dominate the §10.6 "180–220 MB" footprint). Not a real differentiator.</li> </ul> <p><strong>Real risks (concentrated on QUILL's most safety-critical surfaces).</strong></p> <ul> <li><strong>R1 — Native/dynamic dependency resolution (the dominant risk).</strong> Nuitka's static analyzer must find every module and native DLL at build time, and QUILL's stack is full of packages that load native code through <em>runtime</em> discovery that static analysis cannot follow without manual <code>--include-*</code> help: <code>winsdk</code> (WinRT OCR projections are dynamically generated), <code>pyenchant</code> (libenchant + provider DLLs via filesystem plugin paths), <code>prismatoid</code> (the screen-reader announcement bridge), <code>pyttsx3</code> (SAPI5 COM driver discovery), and <code>llama-cpp-python</code> (<code>llama.dll</code> + optional GPU backends). The failure mode is not "won't compile" — it is a build that <strong>succeeds</strong> with a backend <strong>silently missing at runtime</strong>, and the breakages cluster precisely on the accessibility-critical and AI paths QUILL exists to deliver.</li> <li><strong>R2 — Source tests cannot see freeze breakage (a verification gap).</strong> The entire quality bar (ruff, mypy on core/io, GATE-11, A11Y-4, source-contract, headless a11y, SR scenarios) runs against <em>source</em>, never the compiled artifact. Adopting Nuitka therefore <em>mandates a new CI lane</em>: build the standalone bundle and run <code>tests/a11y</code> + an SR scenario <strong>against the built <code>quill.exe</code></strong>. Without it, R1's silent-backend failures ship unverified — a direct violation of the honesty mandate. This is a permanent, slower second test lane.</li> <li><strong>R3 — The v1.1 plugin runtime (a structural collision).</strong> The PRD plugin model (§10.10) loads plugins as <code>.py</code> at runtime; the current "ship interpreter + ship source" model makes that trivial. A compiled binary is a closed world. Nuitka bundles libpython so runtime import of pure-Python plugins is <em>technically</em> possible, but <code>__file__</code>/path semantics differ, plugins importing uncompiled third-party packages fail to resolve, and the clean "drop a <code>.py</code> in <code>plugins/</code>" UX gets fragile. Moot for 1.0 (first-party only, compile them in); a genuine architectural tension to pre-commit to for 1.1.</li> <li><strong>R4 — Data-file/<code>__file__</code> relocation (moderate, auditable).</strong> QUILL discovers bundled data by package-relative paths (<code>quill/data/words_alpha.txt</code>, <code>th_en_US_v2.dat</code>, <code>quill/core/schemas/*.json</code> for atomic-write validation). Nuitka relocates modules, so every <code>Path(__file__).parent / …</code> loader must be verified under the compiled layout — easy to miss for the schema path, which would break <em>saving</em>, not just a feature.</li> <li><strong>R5 — Field-crash debuggability.</strong> Compiled code still yields tracebacks but with muddier line-mapping than interpreted source, slightly harder for a small team's own support. (Commercial "traceback encryption" is the wrong direction for an OSS project that <em>wants</em> readable diagnostics.)</li> </ul> <p><strong>Not real risks (so they are not over-weighted):</strong> wxPython compatibility (Nuitka has a dedicated wx plugin), C-toolchain/compile time (a non-factor for this team), cost/lock-in (free tier only), and onefile AV issues (avoided by folder mode).</p> <p><strong>Net assessment.</strong> The asymmetry is the conclusion: the gains are nice-to-have (snappier launch, cleaner signing) while the risks land on must-not-break (screen-reader announcements, TTS, OCR, AI, plugins). For a screen-reader-first app where a silently-missing SR bridge is a category-defining failure invisible to the current source-based bar, the risk/reward is unfavorable <em>now</em>.</p> <p><strong>Recommendation.</strong> Do <strong>not</strong> adopt for 1.0. Re-evaluate <strong>free Nuitka, standalone only</strong> as a measured 2.0 packaging spike (PKG-1), gated on one empirical question, and explicitly drop Nuitka Commercial. The deciding contest is <em>Nuitka-standalone vs. PyInstaller-one-folder</em>, not Nuitka vs. nothing, and the tiebreaker is dependency-packaging robustness on those five native packages, measured against a built artifact.</p> <table> <thead> <tr> <th>ID</th> <th>Item</th> <th>Area</th> <th>Size</th> <th>Status</th> <th>Acceptance criteria</th> </tr> </thead> <tbody> <tr> <td>PKG-1</td> <td>Freezing spike: free Nuitka <code>--standalone</code> vs. PyInstaller one-folder</td> <td>Packaging</td> <td>L</td> <td>Todo (2.0)</td> <td>A time-boxed, evidence-producing spike. (1) Build QUILL with free Nuitka <code>--standalone</code> (never <code>--onefile</code>), wx plugin enabled, <code>quill</code> package included. (2) Manually resolve native includes for the five hard dependencies — <code>winsdk</code>, <code>pyenchant</code>, <code>prismatoid</code>, <code>pyttsx3</code>, <code>llama-cpp-python</code> — and confirm each backend loads at runtime in the built artifact. (3) Add a CI lane that runs <code>tests/a11y</code> + an SR scenario <strong>against the built <code>quill.exe</code></strong>, not source, and require it green. (4) Verify package-relative data discovery (<code>quill/data/*</code>, <code>quill/core/schemas/*.json</code>) and atomic-write/save under the relocated layout. (5) Compare cold-start time and installed footprint against both the embeddable-Python bundle and a PyInstaller one-folder build. <strong>Go</strong> only if all five native backends resolve and the built-artifact a11y lane passes; otherwise record a clean <strong>No</strong> with evidence. Commercial Nuitka is explicitly out of scope (moot for an MIT public repo).</td> </tr> </tbody> </table> <hr /> <h2 id="part-v-impact-ranking-and-build-order">Part V: Impact ranking and build order</h2> <p>The user's directive is to develop with an impact-first mindset, and to be wise about whether and when to do hardening, security, and refactoring. This part ranks the entire plan, including QUILL's own features, from most impactful to least, and explains the reasoning so the order is clear and defensible. We are building for greatness and high quality, so impact here means lasting value to the user and to the product's standing, not just visible novelty.</p> <h3 id="22-how-impact-is-judged">22. How impact is judged</h3> <p>Two ideas, kept distinct on purpose:</p> <ul> <li>User impact: how much an item improves the real experience of a blind or low-vision writer (and the power user). A delightful, discoverable QUILL key is high user impact; an internal refactor is not.</li> <li>Leverage: how much an item unlocks or de-risks everything else. The quality-gate ladder and the main_frame characterization tests are low direct user impact but very high leverage, because they make all later work faster, safer, and higher quality.</li> </ul> <p>Great products optimize for both. The wise sequence does the small, high-leverage protections first (so nothing later regresses users), then the high user-impact flagship work, then the larger structural and breadth investments, then the long tail. Specifically, on the user's question: yes, hardening and security and refactoring are worth doing, but not all at once and not all first. The cheap, user-protecting parts of hardening (the latent-crash bug fixes and the untrusted-input security) are top-tier because they are small and prevent real harm. The large refactor (decomposing the UI monolith) is high leverage but should come after the flagship value lands and only behind characterization tests, so it never becomes risk for its own sake.</p> <h3 id="23-the-ranked-tiers">23. The ranked tiers</h3> <p>Each tier lists what belongs in it and why, with the value it brings and the high-value outcome it produces. Items are referenced by their backlog IDs. The order follows the build directive: QUILL's own features and the cheap hardening come first, then GLOW, then BITS Whisperer last, with structural health and documentation sequenced around them.</p> <h4 id="tier-1-protect-users-and-unlock-the-team-do-first">Tier 1: Protect users and unlock the team (do first)</h4> <p>These are small, cheap, and either prevent real harm or unlock everything after them. Highest impact-to-effort ratio in the whole plan.</p> <ul> <li>Fix the latent-crash bugs: BUG-1, BUG-2 (real user paths), then BUG-3 through BUG-7. Value: the product cannot crash on a path a user can reach. Outcome: basic trust and a clean baseline.</li> <li>Harden untrusted input and the worst security gaps: SEC-10 and SEC-11 (XML and zip bombs), SEC-1 (executable-path validation), SEC-13 (secret redaction). Value: a malicious or malformed file cannot harm the user. Outcome: a defensible 1.0 safety bar.</li> <li>Stand up the gate ladder: GATE-1 through GATE-9, especially GATE-4 (banned patterns), GATE-9 (no-silent-network), and GATE-3 (scoped typing). Value: quality becomes objective and AI-assisted work accelerates safely. Outcome: nothing regresses users again, and Copilot and agents become a force multiplier instead of a risk.</li> <li>The accessibility gate on every change: GATE-7. Value: the mission becomes machine-enforced. Outcome: no UI change can quietly break a screen reader.</li> <li>Make feature flags a kept promise: FLAG-1 (and FLAG-2 as a definition-of-done check). Value: turning a feature off actually removes it from every surface, with no orphaned commands or dead menu entries. Outcome: users trust that the product respects their choices, and every later feature inherits the same contract for free. The richer profiles UI (FLAG-3) and export (FLAG-4) follow in Tier 2.</li> </ul> <p>Why first: these are inexpensive, they protect users immediately, and they make every later step cheaper and safer. Skipping them would mean building flagship features on sand.</p> <h4 id="tier-2-the-flagship-experience-users-will-talk-about-do-next">Tier 2: The flagship experience users will talk about (do next)</h4> <p>The highest user-impact features: the reasons a blind writer would choose and recommend QUILL.</p> <ul> <li>The QUILL key as a discoverable, tunable, consistent flagship: QK-1 through QK-5, QK-9 (question-mark cheat sheet and per-mode help). Value: the signature interaction becomes learnable in seconds and fast forever. Outcome: a memorable identity no competitor has.</li> <li>Navigation and selection by structure: NAV-1, NAV-4 (go-to-anything), NAV-5, SEL-1 through SEL-3. Value: moving and selecting by meaning, not by guesswork. Outcome: real documents become navigable by ear at speed.</li> <li>Bring visuals into text with OCR: OCR-1 through OCR-5. Value: a blind writer pulls text out of an image, a screenshot, a clipboard grab, or a screen region and hears it land in the document, working offline by default through the built-in Windows OCR engine with no install, and through Tesseract when the user opts in during onboarding. Outcome: inaccessible images stop being dead ends, and the same engine plugs into Watch Profiles so whole folders of images become readable documents automatically.</li> <li>Send files straight to QUILL from the file manager: SHELL-1 through SHELL-3. Value: a blind writer right-clicks an image, PDF, or document in Explorer and chooses Open, OCR, OCR to structured Markdown, or Read aloud, and the running QUILL instance carries it out — the same verbs reachable in-app and from the command line (<code>quill --action ocr <path></code>), all opt-in through the Integration and Context Menu settings. Outcome: QUILL becomes the obvious destination for inaccessible files anywhere on the system, with the classic Explorer menu shipping first (SHELL-1) and the Windows 11 modern-menu packaging and the AI structuring pass tracked honestly as remaining (SHELL-2, SHELL-3).</li> <li>The Accessibility agent and trustworthy AI editing: AGENT-1, AI-7 (diffs reviewable by ear), AI-1 (streaming), AI-6 (graceful degradation). Value: "make this document accessible," done step by step, reversibly, by keyboard and voice. Outcome: a category-defining capability that embodies the mission.</li> <li>Make the configured AI providers actually work: AI-13 (the headline fix), AI-15 (provider correctness), AI-17 (chat-path error messages), then AI-14 (streaming) and AI-16 (contract tests). Value: when a user selects OpenAI, OpenRouter, Gemini, Azure, Ollama, or Claude, that provider is the one that responds, instead of silently falling back to the local model. Outcome: the provider boundary becomes trustworthy and the QUILL AI surface is honest end to end. This is both a QUILL feature and AI hardening, so it sits here with the flagship.</li> <li>Deep, tunable settings: SET-1 through SET-7. Value: users shape QUILL precisely to their hands and ears. Outcome: the product meets people exactly where they are.</li> <li>Feature profiles and per-feature control: FLAG-3 and FLAG-4. Value: users adopt whole sets of features at once (Essential through Full QUILL) or toggle anything individually, with dependencies resolved and announced, and can export or pre-configure the whole set. Outcome: the feature-flag promise from Tier 1 becomes a rich, accessible control surface, so people run exactly the QUILL they want.</li> <li>Trustworthy external-change handling: FEAT-19. Value: a file edited by another program is never silently lost or silently overwritten; clean files reload in place without losing the cursor, and conflicts get a clear spoken choice. Outcome: QUILL feels safe and alive when documents change underneath it, which matters most to a screen-reader user who cannot see a flicker.</li> <li>Watch Profiles, the accessible automation hub: WATCH-1 through WATCH-7 (many folders, each bound to its own action, all feeding one durable, monitorable queue with an accessible Watch Queue Monitor in Help). Value: a blind writer drops work into folders and listens to a calm queue process it — open, convert, move, run a macro or Python transform, or a consented AI action — instead of shepherding every file by hand. Outcome: a productivity-and-accessibility superpower that unifies QUILL's distinctive engines behind one learnable model (a folder, an action, a queue), and a quiet differentiator no mainstream editor offers. The GLOW and transcription actions (WATCH-8, WATCH-9) plug into the same registry as their tiers land.</li> <li>Dialogs that never trap or go silent: DLG-1, enforced by the A11Y-4 contract guard. Value: the safe submit-once forms (find-in-files, Status Bar Layout, the voice chooser, Watch Folder Settings, the YAML editor, General Preferences) move onto the one shared accessible <code>show_web_form</code> surface, and a machine-enforced guard makes the construction bug class behind the find-in-files Cancel trap (#84) and the bookmark dialogs (#85) un-regressable. Outcome: every dialog has a reliable Escape, an explicit default, and returns focus to the editor, and new dialogs are correct by construction. This executes the form wave of issue #73; the interactive AI-tool dialogs are converted individually and screen-reader-verified later (DLG-2, Tier 4), never batch-rewritten.</li> </ul> <p>Why second: this is where user love is won. It comes right after the protections so the flagship is built on a safe, gated base.</p> <h4 id="tier-3-glow-the-accessibility-engine-inside-quill-quill-10--tier-2-scope-sequenced-after-tier-4">Tier 3: GLOW, the accessibility engine inside QUILL (QUILL 1.0 — Tier 2 scope, sequenced after Tier 4)</h4> <blockquote> <p>Status (2026-06-03): <strong>back in QUILL 1.0.</strong> Per maintainer direction the shared <code>quill-glow-core</code> engine requirements are now met (the engine is green), so the GLOW family (GLOW-1 through GLOW-7) returns to the 1.0 milestone, classified under Tier 2 (the flagship experience) and sequenced for execution after the Tier 4 structural work. The GLOW watch-action binding (WATCH-8) and the axe-core / Accessibility Agents workstream (AX-A through AX-F) remain deferred to <strong>QUILL 2.0</strong>. The "Tier 3" heading is retained for continuity with the cross-repo execution plan in <a href="glow.md">glow.md</a>.</p> </blockquote> <p>Per the build directive, GLOW comes before transcription: it deepens QUILL's core mission of accessible documents and reuses a proven engine.</p> <ul> <li>GLOW as the in-app accessibility engine: GLOW-1 through GLOW-5. Step 1 of GLOW-1 is a cross-repo prerequisite: fix the failing CI in the <code>s:\code\glow</code> engine repo and bring it green before QUILL depends on it. Then QUILL audits and fixes real documents to ACB, APH, and WCAG standards in place, through the shared <code>quill-glow-core</code> API rather than the thin local bridge. The work spans three repos (QUILL, <code>quill-glow-core</code>, <code>glow</code>); the stale <code>s:\code\glow-7.0.0</code> snapshot is ignored. Value: writing and document accessibility become one app, built on a stable engine. Outcome: a category-defining, accessibility-native editor.</li> <li>GLOW interaction quality: surface findings as reviewable-by-ear diffs and one-key jumps, reuse the consented, reversible flow from the Accessibility agent (AGENT-1), and keep the standards profiles (ACB 2025 Baseline, APH Submission, Combined Strict) explicit and announced. Value: the audit-and-fix loop is fully accessible. Outcome: GLOW feels native to QUILL, not bolted on.</li> <li>Code-quality and integration fixes for the boundary: replace the thin <code>quill/core/glow.py</code> bridge with calls into <code>quill-glow-core</code>, add contract tests for the shared API, and keep version and component reporting honest. Value: a clean, tested seam. Outcome: GLOW improvements flow into QUILL without surprises.</li> <li>GLOW as a watch action: WATCH-8. Value: register GLOW's audit-and-fix flow as a Watch Profile action so a shared folder of incoming documents is made accessible automatically, reversibly, and announced through the queue. Outcome: the accessibility engine becomes hands-free for whole folders, not just the open document.</li> </ul> <p>Why third: high strategic value that extends the core mission, depends on the gated base (Tier 1), and is most compelling once the flagship experience (Tier 2) already feels great. Most cost is integration, not invention.</p> <h4 id="tier-4-structural-health-and-performance-that-keep-greatness-durable-high-leverage">Tier 4: Structural health and performance that keep greatness durable (high leverage)</h4> <p>Not flashy, but what lets the product stay excellent as it grows.</p> <ul> <li>The largest refactor, done first: DLG-3. Review every single dialog across <code>quill/ui/*</code> and convert any custom-drawn or hand-rolled dialog that could conform to a stock wxPython construct — even an enhanced wxPython standard built on stock controls — into native wxPython. The risk this retires is concrete and otherwise invisible to a Linux/source-contract bar: controls that do not work, focus landing on the wrong control, and rendering breakage all live in custom dialogs. The A11Y-4 dialog-contract guard is strengthened in the same effort so the residual-custom-dialog bug class is caught at author time and cannot regress. This supersedes the batch ambition of DLG-2 (its individual AI-tool conversions fold in) and is sequenced ahead of the monolith decomposition so the dialog estate is sound before CQ-1/CQ-16 split it. Value: dialogs that are correct by construction. Outcome: the single highest-risk surface in the UI becomes durable and verifiable. DLG-3 also folds in and supersedes issues #73 and #72, whose concrete inventory it inherits: <ul> <li><strong>Two sanctioned dialog kinds, nothing bespoke.</strong> <strong>Native</strong> (<code>wx.MessageDialog</code>/<code>wx.MessageBox</code>) for confirms, saves, and simple messages; the existing <strong>accessible web surfaces</strong> for anything that displays rich content or captures input — reuse <code>AccessibleChatView</code> (the Ask Quill chat input pattern), <code>AccessibleHtmlDialog</code> for display, and <code>MarkdownPreviewDialog</code> for previews. No new component is built.</li> <li><strong>Keep as-is (already correct):</strong> <code>AskQuillChatDialog</code> (web chat), <code>MarkdownPreviewDialog</code> (web), the unsaved-changes prompt (native <code>wx.MessageDialog</code>), and the delete-note confirm (native).</li> <li><strong>Convert to web (display or input):</strong> <code>StickyNoteEditorDialog</code> and <code>StickyNotesVaultDialog</code> (<code>quill/ui/sticky_notes.py</code>); <code>RunPythonDialog</code>, <code>PromptStudioDialog</code>, <code>AgentCenterDialog</code>, <code>WritingAssistantDialog</code>, <code>SearchableModelPickerDialog</code>, <code>AIHubDialog</code>, and <code>AssistantConnectionDialog</code> (<code>quill/ui/assistant_tools.py</code>); <code>AIModelDialog</code> (<code>quill/ui/ai_model_panel.py</code>); <code>TrainStyleDialog</code> (<code>quill/ui/style_panel.py</code>); and the onboarding/welcome flow, which must render in a web surface instead of dumping raw HTML into a <code>Document</code> (this fixes #72).</li> <li><strong>Convert to native <code>wx.MessageDialog</code>:</strong> the trust-consent prompt, the untrusted-location prompt, and the assorted confirm prompts in <code>quill/ui/main_frame.py</code>.</li> <li><strong>Decide case-by-case:</strong> <code>CommandPaletteDialog</code> (search + list picker) — likely web for consistent screen-reader behavior.</li> <li><strong>Bugs this closes:</strong> the first-run onboarding <strong>startup crash</strong> on Windows and macOS with a screen reader active (it lives in a hand-rolled onboarding dialog in the <code>show_startup_wizard_page</code> → profile/assistant/speech/watch-folder sequence); the sticky-note editor landing focus on <strong>Save</strong> instead of the text field; and #72's raw-HTML welcome document.</li> <li><strong>Acceptance:</strong> no hand-rolled <code>wx.Dialog</code> subclasses remain for confirm/message flows (those use <code>wx.MessageDialog</code>/<code>MessageBox</code>); input/display dialogs use the existing accessible web surfaces (not a new component); first-run onboarding completes without crashing on Windows and macOS with a screen reader on; welcome/onboarding content renders rather than appearing as raw HTML.</li> </ul></li> <li>Decompose the UI monolith behind characterization tests: CQ-16 then CQ-1, with GATE-6 and GATE-11. Value: every future change gets faster and safer. Outcome: the 18,748-line risk becomes maintainable. Sequencing note: do this after Tier 2 ships and after DLG-3, never as a prerequisite to Tier 2, and always behind passing characterization tests.</li> <li>Drive the strict-typing zone and lint to clean: TYPE-1 through TYPE-8, CQ-18. Value: a class of bugs disappears at author time. Outcome: a codebase that resists regression.</li> <li>Remove first-use stalls and enforce budgets: PERF-1 through PERF-3, PERF-9 through PERF-14, GATE-10. Value: the app feels instant. Outcome: performance that does not slip back.</li> <li>The remaining security hardening: SEC-7, SEC-14 through SEC-17, SEC-8 (plugins stay gated); SEC-6 (download checksum verification) is done. Value: defense in depth. Outcome: a product that is safe by construction.</li> <li>Last code item before documentation: ORG-1, a full source-tree organizational review. Relocate root-level platform files to their sanctioned homes (for example <code>macos_app.py</code>/<code>setup_macos.py</code> into <code>quill/platform/macos/</code>), consolidate scattered root design docs into <code>docs/</code>, and rationalize the build/dist scratch folders into one ignored output convention. Sequenced <strong>dead last among Tier 4 code</strong> because it touches imports, CI paths, installer scripts, and packaging manifests, so it runs only after the other refactors settle and entirely behind the GATE-6/GATE-11/A11Y-4 and full-test gates. Value: a best-in-class repository tree a new contributor can navigate at a glance. Outcome: structure that matches the quality of the code inside it.</li> </ul> <p>Why fourth: high leverage but mostly invisible to users, so it follows the work that wins user love, while the cheap protective parts of it already happened in Tier 1.</p> <h4 id="tier-5-bits-whisperer-transcription-deferred-to-quill-20">Tier 5: BITS Whisperer transcription (deferred to QUILL 2.0)</h4> <blockquote> <p>Status (2026-06-02): deferred to <strong>QUILL 2.0</strong>. BITS Whisperer is the second distinctive engine and the integration plan below stands, but it is no longer part of the 1.0 milestone.</p> </blockquote> <p>Transcription is the second proven engine QUILL absorbs, and with the tier swap it now precedes documentation so the docs can describe a product whose transcription is already built. BITS Whisperer (<code>s:\code\bw</code>) is production-ready (875 tests passing, CI green, a framework-agnostic core with no <code>wx</code> imports), so unlike GLOW there is no engine-repo fix to do first — this tier is pure, clean integration.</p> <ul> <li>The canonical transcript model is the keystone: BW-1. Add <code>quill/core/transcript.py</code> (a <code>Transcript</code> / <code>TranscriptSegment</code> / <code>TranscriptMetadata</code> model) so all of BITS Whisperer's providers, whose raw outputs differ in timestamp and diarization shape, normalize to one structure that GLOW audit, AI clean-up, and export all consume. Value: provider variance never leaks into QUILL. Outcome: one stable shape everything builds on. Everything else in this tier depends on it.</li> <li>Wire the transcription runtime: BW-2, BW-3. Add <code>quill/core/bw_transcription.py</code> (a wrapper binding BITS Whisperer's <code>TranscriptionService</code> / <code>ProviderManager</code> / <code>ModelManager</code> to QUILL's async and announcement model) and <code>quill/io/transcripts.py</code> (JSON↔︎Markdown with speaker labels and timestamps). The existing <code>bw_speech.py</code> model management and <code>bw_providers.py</code> readiness surfaces finally drive real transcription. Value: transcribe audio, then edit, audit, and publish without leaving QUILL. Outcome: a write-transcribe-audit-publish loop no mainstream editor has.</li> <li>Three-tier provider exposure behind feature flags: BW-9. <code>core.bw_providers_local</code> (always on, offline Whisper/Vosk/Windows Speech) → <code>core.bw_providers_plus</code> (opt-in Deepgram, OpenAI, Groq, with cost estimation) → <code>core.bw_providers_enterprise</code> (all 18). Cloud providers are opt-in per action with keys in the OS credential vault; nothing reaches a network silently. Value: progressive disclosure instead of an 18-provider wall. Outcome: a newcomer transcribes locally in two keystrokes; a pro unlocks the full matrix.</li> <li>Transcription as a watch action: WATCH-9. Register BITS Whisperer transcription as a Watch Profile action so dropping audio into a folder produces an editable, accessible document automatically, which the queue can chain into the GLOW (WATCH-8) and export actions. Outcome: the drop-audio-in, accessible-edited-document-out loop becomes fully hands-free and screen-reader-narrated — the most distinctive single payoff of the WATCH family.</li> <li>Right-size and unify the suite: BW-4 (consume BITS Whisperer purely as a library — do NOT absorb its duplicate shell, tray, setup wizard, updater, settings dialog, watch folder, plugin manager, or license/registration; QUILL owns those surfaces), BW-7 (one shared export path), and one Settings home and one update path (Part IV, section 21). Value: less to learn, less to maintain. Outcome: a coherent suite rather than overlapping apps.</li> <li>Defer the far edges (beyond this tier, guided by feedback): live microphone captioning (extends dictation), speaker-turn analysis and meeting minutes, multilingual segmentation, and a transcription cost dashboard. Value: keep the tier focused on the proven loop. Outcome: the headline ships clean; the explorations follow real demand.</li> </ul> <p>Packaging note for 2.0: the freezing decision (PKG-1, §21.2) is a 2.0 packaging spike, not part of this transcription tier. It runs as an independent, evidence-producing evaluation of free Nuitka <code>--standalone</code> vs. PyInstaller one-folder, gated on the five native backends resolving and an a11y lane passing against the built <code>quill.exe</code>. 1.0 ships on the existing embeddable-Python bundle.</p> <p>Why fifth: it is the second category-defining engine and it completes the WATCH loop, but it sits one step further from the writing-and-accessibility core than GLOW, so it follows the flagship, GLOW, and structural work — and now precedes documentation so the docs describe it as finished.</p> <h4 id="tier-6-documentation-greatness-and-the-learning-surface-completes-the-product-last">Tier 6: Documentation greatness and the learning surface (completes the product, last)</h4> <p>A great product is not done until people can learn it — and per the tier swap, documentation lands last so it describes a product that is already built, safe, delightful, GLOW-native, and transcription-capable. The BITS Whisperer docs at <code>s:\code\bw\docs</code> (PRD, USER_GUIDE, GETTING_STARTED) are reused and harmonized into QUILL's voice rather than rewritten from scratch.</p> <ul> <li>Consolidate and elevate the docs folder: DOC-14 through DOC-17, DOC-11 (accessibility), DOC-12 (engineering docs). Value: documentation that is itself a model of accessibility and clarity. Outcome: nobody is left behind.</li> <li>DOC-18 (PRD consolidation and clean polish): bring <code>docs/QUILL-PRD.md</code> fully current with shipped behavior, then roll the entire <code>docs/accessibility/</code> and <code>docs/engineering/</code> document sets up into the PRD as consolidated sections so the PRD is the single source of truth. Once their content lives in the PRD, retire the <code>docs/accessibility/</code> and <code>docs/engineering/</code> folders (and their generated <code>.html</code>/<code>.epub</code> artifacts), updating every cross-reference and the docs-artifact gate accordingly. Regenerate the PRD <code>.html</code>/<code>.epub</code> via pandoc and pass <code>scripts/check_docs_artifacts.py</code> and <code>scripts/plain_language_lint.py</code>. Value: one authoritative, accessible, well-polished PRD instead of scattered folders. Outcome: a clean documentation surface with no duplication or drift.</li> <li>The learning surface: DOC-1 through DOC-8, the podcasts (POD-1 through POD-5), and the tutorials (TUT-1 through TUT-7), now including the transcription and GLOW audit loops as first-class lessons. Value: a complete path from first launch to mastery. Outcome: a launch that lands with a full learning experience.</li> <li>Test breadth: CQ-11 through CQ-16, CQ-23, CQ-24, the IO matrix, and the sandbox policy suite. Value: confidence that everything documented actually works. Outcome: durable quality.</li> <li>Breadth and exploration (folded in here as the final reach): NAV-10 (symbol navigation), AI-11 (local grounded answers), AI-12 (evaluation harness), AI-18 (optional GitHub Copilot SDK provider, only if beta users ask), FEAT-12 through FEAT-18, LINUX-2 (accessible Linux to the product bar), ECO-1 (plugin capability, signing, marketplace), L10N-1 (full UI and docs localization), and COLLAB-1 (accessible asynchronous review and sharing). Value: new reach and polish. Outcome: a growing product shaped by real user feedback, with nothing of substance left below best-in-class.</li> </ul> <h4 id="power-tools-feature-parity-delivered-in-quill-10">Power Tools feature parity (delivered in QUILL 1.0)</h4> <blockquote> <p>Status (2026-06-09): <strong>delivered</strong>. Originally captured as a QUILL 2.0 backlog from a competitive analysis of Power Tools 4.0 (Jamal Mazrui, 2007 to 2017), a respected screen-reader-first Windows text editor (the "Homer editor interface"), this set of self-contained editor conveniences (EDS-1 through EDS-21) was pulled forward and completed for 1.0. Each command's wx-free logic lives in a dedicated <code>quill/core</code> module with unit tests; the UI layer wires them through <code>PowerToolsActionsMixin</code>, registers them on the command palette and Keymap Editor (no default keybinding, to avoid colliding with QUILL's curated keymap), and surfaces them on the <strong>Tools > Power Tools</strong> submenu.</p> </blockquote> <p>Why this matters competitively: Power Tools is one of the few editors built, like QUILL, primarily for blind and low-vision writers, and it accumulated two decades of small, speech-friendly editor conveniences. QUILL already matches or exceeds Power Tools across its entire core editing, navigation, search, snippet, structured text, and file-management surface, and far exceeds it on AI, accessibility audit (GLOW), OCR and image description, modern TTS, dictation, macros, watch folders, feature profiles, and cross-screen-reader announcements. Power Tools's only large category QUILL deliberately does not pursue is RTF <em>live rich-editing</em> (visual word processing on a <code>wx.RichTextCtrl</code>) and JScript.NET scripting add-ins; RTF as a <em>file format</em> is delivered as an io-layer round-trip (EDS-21). The actionable residue — a set of roughly twenty self-contained editor conveniences plus the RTF format work — is listed as EDS-1 through EDS-21 below, all delivered in 1.0.</p> <p><strong>Competitive analysis: Power Tools 4.0 vs QUILL</strong></p> <table> <thead> <tr> <th>Capability area</th> <th>Power Tools 4.0</th> <th>QUILL today</th> <th>Verdict</th> </tr> </thead> <tbody> <tr> <td>Core Done: <code>quill/core/run_target.py</code> (<code>classify_target</code>/<code>target_at_cursor</code>/<code>is_dangerous_executable</code>) covered by <code>tests/unit/core/test_run_target.py</code>; wired as <code>run_current_file</code>/<code>run_target_at_cursor</code> (gated by <code>is_dangerous_executable</code>), registered <code>power.run_current_file</code>/<code>power.run_target_at_cursor</code>.</td> </tr> <tr> <td>EDS-20</td> <td>Rename and delete current file on disk</td> <td>Features</td> <td>S</td> <td>Done</td> <td>Commands rename and delete the current file both in the editor and on disk; rename is guarded by an explicit new-name prompt and delete by a destructive yes/no confirmation; tests cover the wiring and cancel paths. (Power Tools Alt+Shift+R, Alt+Shift+D.) Done: wired as <code>rename_current_file</code> (new-name prompt) and <code>delete_current_file</code> (yes/no confirmation), registered <code>power.rename_current_file</code>/<code>power.delete_current_file</code> on the Power Tools menu and Keymap Editor.</td> </tr> <tr> <td>EDS-21</td> <td>RTF round-trip through the io layer</td> <td>IO</td> <td>M</td> <td>Done</td> <td>Promote RTF from the current lossy extract-only path to a real <code>io/*</code> format that reads RTF formatting into QUILL's Markdown-style internal markup and writes Markdown back out to valid RTF, following the <code>read(path) -> Document</code> / <code>write(doc, path)</code> contract; bold, italic, headings, lists, and links survive a round trip; no change to the editor control surface (the writing path stays a plain-text <code>wx.TextCtrl</code> over markup). Tests cover read, write, and a formatting round-trip. This is the only RTF scope QUILL pursues; see the note below. Done: <code>quill/io/rtf.py</code> implements <code>markdown_to_rtf</code>/<code>rtf_to_markdown</code>/<code>read_rtf_document</code>/<code>write_rtf_document</code>; <code>read_structured_document</code> delegates <code>.rtf</code> to the new reader (the lossy <code>_format_rtf</code> was removed) and <code>MainFrame._write_document_to_disk</code> routes <code>.rtf</code> saves through <code>write_rtf_document</code>; covered by <code>tests/unit/io/test_rtf.py</code> and <code>tests/unit/ui/test_main_frame_rtf_roundtrip.py</code>, strict-mypy and ruff clean.</td> </tr> <tr> <td>RTF-22</td> <td>Optional native Rich text lens (rtf.md Part One)</td> <td>Features</td> <td>L</td> <td>In progress</td> <td>An opt-in alternative editor surface that renders bold/italic/headings/bullets/links natively in a <code>wx.richtext.RichTextCtrl</code>, gated behind the <code>editor_surface</code> setting (default <code>plain</code>) so the stock <code>wx.TextCtrl</code> stays the default writing path. QUILL Markdown remains the canonical document value, so every existing offset-based command, search, metric, outline, autosave, and persistent-undo keeps working unchanged through the surface. <strong>Delivered:</strong> wx-free rich model and conversions (<code>quill/io/rtf_model.py</code>), RTF safety scanning that strips OLE/object groups and flags remote fields before native loading (<code>quill/io/rtf_safety.py</code>, wired into <code>read_rtf_document</code>), spoken-format vocabulary (<code>quill/core/format_speech.py</code>), the editor-surface protocol (<code>quill/ui/editor_surface.py</code>), the dual-lens <code>RichTextSurface</code> with a read-only native rich view plus the canonical Markdown lens (<code>quill/ui/rich_text_surface.py</code>), lossless lens switching (<code>view.switch_editing_lens</code>, <code>Ctrl+Shift+Grave, K</code>), and <code>.rtf</code> files opening in the rich lens when enabled. All covered by wx-free unit/source-contract tests, strict-mypy and ruff clean. <strong>In progress (honest):</strong> an editable rich surface with rich-native persistent undo — the rich lens currently renders read-only and editing happens in the Markdown lens, because faithful structural reconstruction from a live <code>RichTextCtrl</code> buffer plus rich undo is the subtlest part of the proposal and is not yet complete.</td> </tr> </tbody> </table> <p>Note on RTF and the editor control surface: QUILL's writing path is a stock <code>wx.TextCtrl</code> on purpose, because plain-text-first editing gives the strongest, most predictable screen-reader fidelity (NVDA, JAWS, Narrator parity) and is a standing design rule. There are two distinct "RTF support" scopes and only one is pursued. <strong>In scope (EDS-21):</strong> RTF as a <em>format</em> in the io layer, where formatting is read into QUILL's Markdown-style markup and written back out to RTF, with the editor surface unchanged. This is a clean extension of the existing <code>io/*</code> contract and the right home for the lossy <code>_format_rtf</code> extract that exists today. <strong>Out of scope by design:</strong> RTF as a <em>live rich-editing surface</em> on the default writing path. The stock <code>wx.TextCtrl</code> remains the default and only writing control, because plain-text-first editing gives the strongest screen-reader fidelity and every existing command and EDS-1..20 convenience assumes it. RTF-22 adds an <strong>opt-in</strong> Rich text lens (off by default) that renders formatting natively in a <code>wx.richtext.RichTextCtrl</code> <em>without</em> changing the canonical document model — QUILL Markdown stays the editable canonical value, the rich view is an overlay, and the two lenses switch losslessly. A fully editable rich surface with rich-native undo remains honestly in progress (see RTF-22). Importantly, none of EDS-1 through EDS-20 depend on RTF; they are plain-text conveniences that ship on the current surface regardless of whether EDS-21 lands.</p> <p>Status: delivered in 1.0. Every EDS item is a self-contained editor convenience, implemented with its wx-free logic in a dedicated <code>quill/core</code> module (unit-tested) and wired into the editor through <code>PowerToolsActionsMixin</code>, the command palette, the Keymap Editor, and the Tools > Power Tools submenu. The two large Power Tools categories QUILL omits, RTF <em>live</em> word processing and JScript.NET scripting, remain deliberate scope choices rather than backlog items (see the RTF note above; EDS-21 delivers RTF only as an io-layer file format).</p> <h4 id="20-competitive-parity-plan-notepad-benchmark-adapted-for-quill">2.0 competitive parity plan (Notepad++ benchmark, adapted for QUILL)</h4> <p>This plan captures the highest-value competitive gaps identified in the Notepad++ comparison and folds them into QUILL 2.0 without violating QUILL's accessibility and trust principles.</p> <ol type="1"> <li><strong>Search at project scale (COMP-1).</strong> Deliver a first-class Find in Folder and Workspace Search flow so technical writers and developer users can search and replace across real projects without leaving QUILL. The key requirement is not just speed; it is accessibility stability: results must be reviewable by ear, keyboard traversal must never lose context, and operation summaries must announce exactly what changed.</li> <li><strong>Workspace depth, not just session recall (COMP-2).</strong> Expand existing session support into an explicit workspace model with named sets, folder trees, and project groups, including reliable restore announcements. This is the core context-switching parity move: users should reopen "what I was working on" in one action with deterministic focus and state restoration.</li> <li><strong>Plugin lifecycle parity, but safer than competitors (COMP-3).</strong> Build a complete plugin management lifecycle that users can operate fully by keyboard and screen reader, while preserving QUILL's stricter consent and capability boundaries. The bar is functional parity with Notepad++ plugin administration plus stronger security posture (permission prompts, provenance, rollback, and safe-mode fallback).</li> <li><strong>Encoding conversion as an explicit workflow (COMP-4).</strong> Close the known encoding gap by adding convert flows, loss-risk preview, and reversible outcomes, not just open or reload choices. This targets legacy-document and mixed-encoding workflows where blind users currently need external tools to recover text safely.</li> <li><strong>Macro power-user maturity (COMP-5).</strong> Move macros from basic utility to a robust automation surface with naming, descriptions, repeat controls, import/export, and deterministic replay behavior. The objective is a "safe automation" model where productivity gains do not come at the cost of unpredictable edits or silent failures.</li> <li><strong>Two-pane editing for review-heavy workflows (COMP-6).</strong> Add optional split editing that remains stock-control and announcement-correct, enabling side-by-side review and synchronized navigation. This is specifically scoped for compare and revision tasks and must preserve QUILL's no-focus-chaos rule.</li> <li><strong>GitHub Copilot SDK as an optional cloud provider (COMP-7).</strong> Add a GitHub Copilot adapter behind the existing <code>quill/ai/*</code> provider boundary and consent/egress gates (AI-13, GATE-9), never a default and off until the user opts in. It reuses the device-login sign-in path (AI-19) so a user can authenticate with a subscription they already have instead of pasting a hidden API key, and it explicitly targets Copilot's <strong>free tier</strong> so the capability is reachable without a paid plan. Strictly local-first defaults are preserved: the bundled GGUF models stay the default, no document content leaves the device without per-action consent, and the adapter advertises an unavailable path with an announced reason when the user is not signed in. The subscription/sign-in friction is the reason this stays post-1.0 rather than a core capability.</li> </ol> <p>Intentional non-goal in this parity plan: <strong>multi-caret and rectangular editing remain out of scope</strong> for QUILL's primary interaction model, because they conflict with screen-reader navigation patterns and the plain, predictable edit-field-first philosophy.</p> <p>Why sixth: essential for greatness, but it should describe a product that is already built, safe, delightful, and feature-complete, so it follows everything it documents.</p> <p>Why last: valuable but not required for a great, trustworthy 1.0; the transcription suite and the stretch items are best chosen with beta evidence in hand, and the build directive explicitly places transcription after QUILL, hardening, and GLOW.</p> <h3 id="24-the-development-mindset-in-one-paragraph">24. The development mindset in one paragraph</h3> <p>Lead with cheap protections and gates, because they make everything after them safe and fast. Then spend the bulk of the energy on the flagship QUILL experience, including making the configured AI providers actually respond (AI-13), because that is where greatness is felt and where the product must be honest. Bring GLOW in next, since it extends QUILL's accessibility mission and reuses a proven engine, and only then add BITS Whisperer transcription, which is the most distant from the writing core. Treat the big refactor and performance and security depth as high-leverage investments that follow, not precede, the user-facing wins, and always do the refactor behind characterization tests. On the GitHub Copilot SDK: keep it optional and post-1.0, behind the same provider boundary, never a default, because its subscription and sign-in friction conflict with the local-first, bring-your-own-key promise even though the underlying models add value. Finish by making the documentation and learning surface as excellent as the product. Do not do hardening or refactoring for their own sake or all at once; do the parts that protect users early, and the parts that only reshape code later, once their value is clear and their risk is contained.</p> <hr /> <h2 id="part-vi-state-of-the-union">Part VI: State of the union</h2> <p>If every item in this plan were completed (Parts I through V: the delight program, the verified bug and security and typing and performance fixes, the documentation greatness work, the quality-gate ladder, the AI and agent program, the combined GLOW and BITS Whisperer suite, and the impact-ordered build sequence), here is the scale and standing the product would reach.</p> <h3 id="live-progress-log-honest-updated-as-each-major-section-lands">Live progress log (honest, updated as each major section lands)</h3> <p>This log is the single honest record of where the product actually stands as work completes. It is updated at the end of every major section so the team always meets at a truthful assessment, not an aspirational one. Newest entry first.</p> <h4 id="tier-completion-tracker-remaining-backlog-items-per-tier">Tier completion tracker (remaining backlog items per tier)</h4> <p>This table tracks how many of the backlog IDs each tier names are still open. The full source-tree organizational review landed as a sequence of mechanical, behavior-preserving moves with every gate green:</p> <ul> <li><p><strong>Platform files relocated.</strong> <code>macos_app.py</code> → <code>quill/platform/macos/macos_app.py</code> (a thin <code>from quill.__main__ import main</code> re-export behind the existing <code>quill/platform/*</code> contract, mirroring <code>quill/platform/windows/*</code>) and <code>setup_macos.py</code> → <code>scripts/setup_macos.py</code>. <code>pyproject.toml</code> and <code>scripts/build_macos.sh</code> were repathed in the same change, and <code>docs/engineering/macos-build.{md,html,epub}</code> documents the new layout.</p></li> <li><p><strong>Root design docs consolidated.</strong> <code>aa</code>, <code>glow</code>, <code>pi</code>, and the project hub <code>ROADMAP</code> (<code>.md</code> plus regenerated <code>.html</code>/<code>.epub</code>) moved into <code>docs/planning/</code>. All 55 internal relative links in <code>ROADMAP.md</code> and every external reference (the copilot instructions, the blocked-items and dialog-estate engineering docs, and the structure guard) were repointed in step; the docs-artifact gate stays green.</p></li> <li><p><strong>Single ignored-output convention.</strong> <code>build/</code>, <code>release-dist-*</code>, <code>installer-smoke*</code>, <code>windows-distribution</code>, and <code>release-artifacts*</code> are all covered by <code>.gitignore</code> with <strong>zero tracked files</strong> — no dead or duplicated tracked scratch artifacts remain.</p></li> <li><p><strong>Repo-wide Markdown health.</strong> A new root <code>.markdownlint-cli2.jsonc</code> (read by both <code>markdownlint-cli2</code> and the VS Code Problems pane) plus a full cleanup brings every tracked <code>.md</code> to <strong>0 lint errors</strong>: MD040 fenced-code languages, MD049/MD050 emphasis style, MD056 table-column counts, MD010 hard tabs, MD005/MD007 list indentation, MD022/MD025/MD028/MD032 spacing, and MD012/MD047 trailing-newline fixes across the PRD, scripting, accessibility, and QA docs and the contributor instructions. A <code>--fix</code> prose-corruption hazard was caught and reverted (a wrapped <code>+</code> conjunction misread as a list bullet). id="2026-06-05-agent-1-accessibility-agent--deterministic-engine-and-reviewable-single-undo-ui-landed">2026-06-05: AGENT-1 accessibility agent — deterministic engine and reviewable, single-undo UI landed</h4> <p>The headline "make this document accessible" agent moved from Todo to <strong>In progress</strong>.</p> <ul> <li>New wx-free, strict-typed core engine <code>quill/core/accessibility_agent.py</code> builds on the in-repo <code>glow.py</code> audit/fix primitives and <code>plain_language.py</code>. <code>build_plan(...)</code> audits four domains — structure (markdown heading spacing to auto, heading-jump to advisory, html missing <code>lang</code> to auto), alt text (markdown/html missing alt to advisory), link text (generic "click here" link text to advisory), and plain language (controlled-vocabulary swaps to auto, case-preserving) — and returns an ordered, per-step-consented plan. <code>apply_plan(plan, text, accepted_ids)</code> applies only the accepted auto-fixable steps, re-audits via <code>audit_text</code>, and returns an <code>AgentRunResult</code> with a run report and before/after finding counts.</li> <li>New <code>AccessibilityAgentDialog</code> (<code>quill/ui/assistant_tools.py</code>) surfaces the plan as an accessible <code>wx.CheckListBox</code> (auto-fixable steps pre-checked, advisory steps listed for review) with a read-only before/after details pane and an Apply Checked Steps button. <code>make_document_accessible()</code> in <code>main_frame.py</code> announces the spoken summary, runs the dialog, and on apply replaces the whole editor buffer with one <code>editor.Replace(0, last, text)</code> so the change is a <strong>single undo</strong>, then opens the re-audit report in a named scratch tab. Reachable at Tools > AI > Make This Document Accessible... (command <code>tools.ai_accessibility_agent</code>); it is deterministic and offline, so it stays outside the AI-enable gate.</li> <li>Tests: 14 core engine tests (<code>tests/unit/core/test_accessibility_agent.py</code>) plus two dialog-apply behavior tests. Quality gates green: ruff format/check clean, strict mypy clean on the new core module, the menu and dialog contract guards pass, <code>dialogs.md</code> gained a row for the new dialog.</li> <li><strong>Honest remaining work:</strong> structure and plain language are auto-fixed, but alt-text and link-text are advisory-only (flagged, not auto-authored), so AGENT-1 stays <strong>In progress</strong> rather than Done. Each validates its options, returns <code>done</code>/<code>failed</code>/<code>skipped</code> outcomes that route through the queue lifecycle, and offers a <code>preview</code>. Open, Move, Copy, and the sandboxed Python transform are fully functional; Convert, Run macro, and consented AI are implemented and unit-tested at the registry seam and wired through <code>WatchService</code>/<code>default_registry</code>, awaiting the UI to supply their handlers (the same callback pattern Open already uses). WATCH-7 stays <strong>In progress</strong>.</li> <li>WATCH-6 moved from Todo to <strong>In progress</strong>. The consent gate now lives in <code>WatchActionRegistry.run</code> (any <code>requires_consent</code> action — today the AI action — returns an announced <code>skipped</code> outcome until the profile carries explicit <code>options["consent"] is True</code>, honoring AI-5's no-silent-network promise), and a side-effect-free <code>WatchActionRegistry.dry_run(...)</code> returns a plain-language preview of what a profile would do or the reason it would not (unknown action, disabled feature, invalid options, missing consent). The sandboxed Python transform honors a clamped wall-clock cap shared with SEC-9, and the single-threaded worker already serializes processing (concurrency of one). Remaining for WATCH-6: the per-profile consent UI that shows provider/model/scope, surfacing the dry-run preview in the profile manager, and announcing resource-cap termination. Twenty new core tests cover the five actions plus the consent gate and dry-run; <code>test_watch_actions.py</code> is 34 passing and the full <code>-k watch</code> core suite is 93 passing. ruff format and check clean.</li> <li>Count reconciliation: WATCH-6/WATCH-7 are In progress, not Done, so no row closed and the <strong>done count is unchanged</strong>. Those historical entries are left as-is as a point-in-time record; the tracker table is canonical going forward.</li> </ul> <h4 id="2026-06-04-per-setting-reset-buttons-completed-set-6">2026-06-04: Per-setting Reset buttons completed SET-6</h4> <p>Code shipped. Importing reapplies accelerators and rebuilds the menu, and the outcome is announced. Tier 2 drops to 36 remaining.</li> <li>SEC-2 closed as a low-risk, pure-core security win: a shared <code>resolve_within</code> helper (with a <code>PathEscapeError</code>) in <code>quill/core/storage.py</code> resolves a candidate path and refuses any target that escapes a permitted base directory (via <code>..</code>, an absolute path, or a symlink-resolved escape). <code>write_json_atomic</code> gained an opt-in <code>base</code> parameter that runs the guard before writing, and the pure-core app-data JSON writers (<code>recent</code>, <code>search-history</code>, <code>palette-usage</code>, <code>features</code>, <code>menu_customization</code>) now pass <code>base=app_data_dir()</code> so they can never be tricked into writing outside the application data area. User-driven exports (<code>.qsf</code> settings, <code>.qpf</code> profiles) deliberately stay unguarded because they target user-chosen locations. Four new unit tests prove traversal is blocked and in-base writes still succeed. Being a cross-cutting protection rather than a tier-listed ID, it does not move the tier counts.</li> <li>CQ-14 and CQ-19 closed in the same <code>storage.py</code> pass: CQ-14 (path-safety tests) is satisfied by the SEC-2 traversal tests, and CQ-19 hardens the atomic write itself — <code>os.replace</code> now runs through a small <code>_atomic_replace</code> retry loop (five attempts, 50 ms backoff) that absorbs the transient <code>PermissionError</code> Windows raises when an antivirus scanner, backup agent, or screen-reader hook briefly holds the destination open, then re-raises if the lock persists. Two new tests simulate transient contention (succeeds after retries) and permanent contention (re-raises). Both are cross-cutting code-quality rows and do not move the tier counts.</li> <li>CQ-20 closed as a pure-core quick win: version pre-release ordering is now intentional and tested. <code>_version_tuple</code> in <code>quill/core/updates.py</code> previously stripped non-digits per segment, so <code>1.2.0-rc1</code> parsed as patch <code>1</code> and wrongly outranked the final <code>1.2.0</code>. It now separates the pre-release suffix from the <code>major.minor.patch</code> core and appends a stage rank so that, for the same triplet, a final build sorts after every pre-release and the stages order final > rc > beta > alpha (unknown suffixes fall to the earliest stage). Five new tests in <code>tests/unit/core/test_updates.py</code> cover stage ordering, the suffix-leak regression, and <code>select_latest</code> preferring the final release. Cross-cutting code quality, so the tier counts are unchanged.</li> <li>A security-hardening triplet closed across the subprocess and credential surfaces. SEC-4 and SEC-15 hardened <code>run_subprocess_safely</code> (<code>quill/stability/safe_subprocess.py</code>): it now rejects an empty <code>args</code> sequence and a <code>cwd</code> that is not an existing directory (both <code>ValueError</code>), documents the safety contract that callers pass a trusted absolute directory, and wraps a launch <code>OSError</code>/<code>FileNotFoundError</code> into a clearly logged, re-raised <code>OSError</code> naming the tool. SEC-16 added a shared <code>validate_credential_identifier</code> (<code>quill/platform/credential_validation.py</code>) that rejects empty, over-long, control-character, and leading-dash identifiers, then wired it into the Windows credential manager (<code>target_name</code>) and the macOS keychain (<code>account</code>/<code>service</code>); the macOS <code>set_secret</code> already checked its return code. SEC-15 and SEC-16 are Tier 4 items, so Tier 4 moves to 11 of 30 done (19 remaining) and the total to 47 of 180; SEC-4 is a cross-cutting documentation row and is not tier-counted. Eleven new unit tests (five subprocess, six credential) cover the new guards.</li> <li>CQ-22 closed alongside the credential-validation work: <code>load_generic_credential</code> in <code>quill/platform/windows/credential_manager.py</code> now documents and debug-logs the two "no usable secret" outcomes so callers can tell them apart — an <strong>absent</strong> credential returns <code>None</code>, while a stored entry with an <strong>empty</strong> blob returns a <code>StoredCredential</code> whose <code>secret</code> is <code>""</code>. A new Windows-only test (<code>tests/unit/platform/windows/test_credential_manager.py</code>) proves the distinction with a real Credential Manager round-trip (absent then None, empty then <code>""</code>, deleted then None again) and that identifier validation still rejects a leading-dash target. Cross-cutting code quality, so the tier counts are unchanged.</li> <li>CQ-17 closed as a pure-documentation win: a new engineering note, <a href="../engineering/thread-safety.md">docs/engineering/thread-safety.md</a>, captures the cache-locking invariants that were previously only implied by the code. It records the two patterns Quill uses — double-checked module-level locking with an immutable published snapshot for read-mostly caches (the spell-check wordlist and enchant handle behind <code>_BACKEND_LOCK</code>, the thesaurus index behind <code>_LOAD_LOCK</code>), and a per-instance lock taken around every access for genuinely mutable state (the watch-folder seen-set) — plus the stability helpers and a rule that forbids new unguarded module-level mutable caches in <code>quill/core</code>. No code changed, so the tier counts are unchanged.</li> <li>CQ-15 closed as the natural follow-on to that note, and is the first Tier 6 item to land. <code>tests/unit/core/test_cache_concurrency.py</code> turns the documented invariants into an executable guard: it cold-starts the thesaurus index and the spell-check wordlist, then hammers <code>thesaurus.lookup</code> and <code>spellcheck.is_known_word</code>/<code>backend_info</code> from sixteen threads released together on a barrier. The tests prove the double-checked lock collapses the thundering herd to a single expensive load (the thesaurus parser runs once; the wordlist file is read at most once) and that every thread observes the same published snapshot and the same active backend tier. The shared note from CQ-17 satisfies the "short note of the invariants" half. CQ-15 is a Tier 6 ID, so Tier 6 moves to 1 of 33 done (32 remaining) and the total to 48 of 180. Note on sequencing: the <em>content</em> documentation (tutorials, podcasts, user guides) is deliberately held until the features they describe are built — closing CQ-15 takes a no-risk test item that happens to live in Tier 6 without prematurely documenting moving targets.</li> <li>CQ-12 and CQ-13 followed as the next two no-risk Tier 6 test items, both exercising already-stable code. CQ-13 ("IO format coverage") confirmed Markdown, HTML, JSON, CSV, DOCX bridge, and PDF scoring all have fixture-based tests: JSON/CSV/DOCX-bridge/PDF-scoring were already covered in <code>tests/unit/io/test_structured.py</code>, and Markdown/HTML fixtures were added to <code>tests/unit/io/test_text.py</code> (they open verbatim through the plain-text reader and round-trip through the writer). CQ-12 ("Undo and redo tests") grew <code>tests/unit/core/test_undo_store.py</code> to cover the edge cases — missing-history load, no-op clear, non-string/non-list payload filtering, the <code>limit</code> boundary (including a non-positive limit coerced to one), the returned bounded list, multi-cycle accumulation, and per-path isolation. CQ-11 ("Spell-check fallback and preload tests") was only half-closeable: its tier-fallback half landed as <code>tests/unit/core/test_spellcheck_backend.py</code>, which pins each of the three backend tiers (enchant, bundled wordlist, stub) and proves <code>backend_info</code>/<code>is_known_word</code> honour the active tier, but the background-preload half stays open because no spell-check preload API exists yet (tracked by PERF-1). With CQ-12 and CQ-13 done, Tier 6 moves to 3 of 33 (30 remaining) and the total to 50 of 180; CQ-11 remains Todo.</li> <li>SET-1 advanced (still In progress) with a zero-risk registry pass: three already-wired <code>Settings</code> fields that had behavior but no Settings-dialog presence were surfaced as registry specs — <code>auto_side_preview</code> (open preview beside the editor) plus the <code>csv_open_mode</code> and <code>word_open_mode</code> open-as choices.
