No behavior changed and no <code>main_frame.py</code> edits were needed; snippets, macros, sticky notes, and status-bar registry specs still keep SET-1 open, so the tier counts are unchanged.</li> </ul> <h4 id="2026-06-01-planning-watch-profiles-scoped-and-feature-flag-respect-made-fundamental">2026-06-01 (planning): Watch Profiles scoped, and feature-flag respect made fundamental</h4> <p>No code shipped in this entry; it records two planning decisions committed to <code>golden.md</code> (and <code>golden.html</code>) before Tier 2 begins.</p> <ul> <li>Watch Profiles (the WATCH family, WATCH-1 through WATCH-9) turns today's single watch-folder into a personal, accessible automation hub: many named profiles, each watching its own folder and bound to its own action (Open, convert or export, move or archive, run a macro, run a sandboxed Python transform, consented AI actions, plus GLOW "audit and fix" and BITS Whisperer "transcribe" as their tiers land), all feeding one durable, restart-safe queue with an accessible Watch Queue Monitor in Help. The core seven items are Tier 2 (flagship); the GLOW action is Tier 3 and the transcription action is Tier 5, plugging into the same registry. This is positioned as a key differentiator because it is simultaneously a productivity and an accessibility superpower and it unifies QUILL's distinctive engines behind one learnable model: a folder, an action, a queue.</li> <li>Feature flags were elevated from an implementation detail to a stated fundamental (new product-wide delight theme 7) backed by the FLAG family. The codebase already has a mature <code>FeatureManager</code> (feature ids, dependencies, maturity, privacy, profiles, command-visibility filtering, status-bar gating); FLAG-1 makes "disabled feature is gone from every surface" a tested UI contract, FLAG-2 makes shipping a <code>FeatureDefinition</code> part of the definition of done, and FLAG-3/FLAG-4 add the rich feature-profile UI and export. FLAG-1 and FLAG-2 are foundational and were added to Tier 1 (its only remaining items); FLAG-3 and FLAG-4 are Tier 2. The entire Watch Profiles surface is specified to honor these flags, so users can run anything from an Open-only setup to a full automation hub purely by toggling features, with the UI always matching their choices.</li> </ul> <p>Backlog totals moved from 142 to 155 (WATCH-1..9 and FLAG-1..4); Tier 1 is now 20 of 22 (the gate ladder and protections complete, FLAG-1/FLAG-2 open), and Tier 2 grew to 38 items.</p> <h4 id="2026-06-01-planning-glow-and-bits-whisperer-gap-analysis-agreed-decisions-and-the-docsbits-tier-swap">2026-06-01 (planning): GLOW and BITS Whisperer gap analysis, agreed decisions, and the docs/BITS tier swap</h4> <p>A grounded read of the three integration repos (<code>s:\code\quill-glow-core</code>, <code>s:\code\glow</code>, <code>s:\code\bw</code>) produced a gap analysis and five agreed decisions that this revision encodes.</p> <ul> <li>The failing CI in <code>s:\code\glow</code> is in the Flask web app (<code>acb_large_print_web</code>), not the engine QUILL consumes: the desktop core (<code>acb_large_print</code> / <code>acb_large_print_core</code>, the <code>from_glow_backend</code> target) already passes 308 tests. Decision: full good-citizen cleanup — fix the web suite, reconcile the version sweep (VERSION 8.0.0 vs README vs TODO.md 2.8.0), and keep the desktop core green. GLOW-1 step 1 is rescoped accordingly.</li> <li>Structured-format audit (DOCX, PPTX, XLSX, PDF, EPUB) is the Tier 3 headline, not a deferred item: GLOW-2 is in scope, since "open a real document and audit it in place" is the whole point and the engine already supports it.</li> <li>Feature-flag respect extends to BITS providers: three tiers (<code>core.bw_providers_local</code> always on, <code>core.bw_providers_plus</code> opt-in, <code>core.bw_providers_enterprise</code> all 18). BW-9 was rewritten from "right-size the set" to this tiered model.</li> <li>BITS Whisperer is healthy (875 tests passing, CI green, framework-agnostic core), so Tier 5 has no engine-repo fix — pure integration. The canonical <code>quill/core/transcript.py</code> model (BW-1) is the keystone the rest of the tier depends on.</li> <li>New GLOW items: GLOW-6 now explicitly surfaces engine/rule version in <code>diagnostics.py</code> and About; GLOW-7 adds a consent gate so GLOW's optional networked AI features are off by default and honor the no-silent-network rule and the GATE-9 egress audit.</li> </ul> <p>Tier swap: per the standing request, BITS Whisperer transcription moves to Tier 5 and documentation to Tier 6, so the docs land last and describe a product that is already GLOW-native and transcription-capable, reusing the <code>s:\code\bw\docs</code> material. The roadmap prose (section 23), the tier tracker, and the WATCH cross-references were all updated. Backlog totals moved 156 -> 159 (GLOW-6 counted, GLOW-7 new, BW-10 counted). Execution is authorized for Tiers 2 through 6 end to end, keeping <code>main</code> green throughout.</p> <h4 id="bookmark-accessibility-agents-integration-research-deferred-to-quill-20">Bookmark: Accessibility Agents integration (research, deferred to QUILL 2.0)</h4> <blockquote> <p>Decision (2026-06-02): deferred to <strong>QUILL 2.0</strong>. The axe-core / Accessibility Agents workstream (AX-A through AX-F) builds on the GLOW engine and report surface, and GLOW is itself a 2.0 item, so this whole workstream follows GLOW into 2.0. The research and design below stand; nothing here is part of the 1.0 milestone, and the tier-renumbering it describes does not apply to 1.0.</p> </blockquote> <p>A research evaluation of the Community Access "Accessibility Agents" project (<code>s:\code\agents</code>, MIT-licensed) is written up in full in <a href="aa.md">aa.md</a> under <code>docs/planning/</code>. The maintainer is inclined to adopt it. If approved, it becomes a NEW Tier 4 inserted immediately after Tier 3 (GLOW), which renumbers the current tiers: Tier 4 (structural health and performance) -> Tier 5, Tier 5 (BITS Whisperer) -> Tier 6, and Tier 6 (documentation) -> Tier 7.</p> <p>The project is about 80 LLM-driven accessibility agents across five host platforms (Claude Code, Copilot, Gemini, Codex, and a Node.js MCP server with 24 scanning tools). Most of it targets web developers and CI and is out of scope for a desktop writing app; the value to QUILL is narrower but real, and it already ships a GLOW bridge, so it is designed to interoperate with Tier 3, not compete. The proposed work (provisional prefix <code>AX-</code>) is six workstreams, all building on the shared GLOW engine and all behind <code>FeatureManager</code> (FLAG-1), reusing the consented AI layer (AI-13), and honoring the no-silent-network rule (GATE-9):</p> <ul> <li>AX-A: adopt the desktop / wxPython / screen-reader knowledge agents (<code>wxpython-specialist</code>, <code>desktop-a11y-specialist</code>, <code>desktop-a11y-testing-coach</code>) as curated contributor guidance in repo memory and instructions. Zero runtime risk; pure documentation; can land even if the rest is declined.</li> <li>AX-B: an optional, off-by-default local MCP audit backend for structured formats (DOCX, PPTX, XLSX, PDF, EPUB, Markdown) that normalizes into the GLOW-3 report surface, adopted only where additive to GLOW. Adds an optional (never hard) Node.js dependency with a clean "backend unavailable" path.</li> <li>AX-C: expose QUILL's own GLOW engine through the project's existing GLOW-bridge contract so the wider ecosystem can audit and fix through QUILL, gated by the GLOW-7 consent rules.</li> <li>AX-D: consented agent-assisted remediation (alt text, plain-language rewrites) folded into the Accessibility agent (AGENT-1) flagship, never a second AI network path; explicit, announced, reviewable apply-and-undo, off by default.</li> <li>AX-E: an axe-core / WCAG 2.2 AA web accessibility check on the HTML a user authors or exports, plus the document-level Web Accessibility specialists (<code>aria-specialist</code>, <code>keyboard-navigator</code>, <code>contrast-master</code>, <code>forms-specialist</code>, <code>modal-specialist</code>, <code>live-region-controller</code>, <code>alt-text-headings</code>, <code>tables-data-specialist</code>, <code>link-checker</code>, <code>i18n-accessibility</code>) as the consented explain-and-fix layer. Decisive technical finding: axe-core needs no Node.js — it is a single self-contained <code>axe.min.js</code> (about half a megabyte, bundled, so zero per-scan bandwidth) injected into QUILL's already-shipped off-screen Edge WebView2 (<code>wx-accessible-webview</code>). The scan is therefore in-process, local, and a SILENT REVIEW by construction: the WebView is created off-screen and never shown, so a background or watch-folder check runs with no UI at all; the navigable findings dialog (GLOW-3) appears only on demand. The optional Node backend (AX-B) is needed only for the heavier Playwright behavioural passes, not the everyday HTML check.</li> <li>AX-F: an optional, off-by-default HTML/CSS/SVG validity check on the HTML a user authors or exports, using the Nu Html Checker (vnu, <code>validator/validator</code>, MIT-licensed), normalized into the GLOW-3 report surface. This is the validity complement to AX-E's axe-core accessibility check: axe-core answers "is this accessible," vnu answers "is this well-formed, standards-conformant HTML," and malformed markup is a common upstream cause of assistive-technology failures. Decisive technical finding: vnu needs no Node.js — it is a Java tool shipped as a self-contained precompiled Windows binary (<code>vnu-runtime-image</code>) that bundles its own Java runtime, so it adds no Node and no separate Java install. It runs as a local command-line check (or an optional local HTTP service) over the generated doc artifacts and any user HTML export, invoked the same way pandoc is — from <code>scripts/</code>/<code>tools/</code>, never inside the pure <code>quill/core</code> tree. Local and silent by construction: no network, results folded into the navigable findings dialog (GLOW-3) on demand. Pairs naturally with the existing <code>check_docs_artifacts.py</code> gate over QUILL's pandoc-generated <code>*.html</code>.</li> </ul> <p>Sequencing: this tier lands after Tier 3 (GLOW) because AX-B, AX-C, AX-D, AX-E, and AX-F all build on the shared GLOW engine and its report surface; documentation still lands last so it describes a product that is already GLOW-native, agent-assisted, and transcription-capable. Open questions for the maintainer are in <a href="aa.md">aa.md</a> section 6. Do not start this work or renumber the tiers until the maintainer confirms after reviewing <a href="aa.md">aa.md</a>.</p> <h4 id="continuation-brief-resume-point-for-the-next-agent-or-session">Continuation brief (resume point for the next agent or session)</h4> <p>Tier 1's original scope is complete: all seven bugs, the cheap security hardening, and the full gate ladder (GATE-1 through GATE-9) are committed and enforced in three CI workflows (<code>pr-ci.yml</code>, <code>security-ci.yml</code>, <code>accessibility-ci.yml</code>). The working tree is gate-clean: <code>ruff format --check .</code> passes, <code>ruff check . --ignore E501</code> passes, <code>mypy quill\core quill\io</code> reports zero errors, and the full suite is 701 passed / 7 skipped with 72.28% core+io coverage. Two foundational feature-flag items (FLAG-1, FLAG-2) were subsequently added to Tier 1 and are open; the richer feature-profile UI (FLAG-3, FLAG-4) sits in Tier 2.</p> <p>First task before anything else (OPS-1): make <code>main</code> green. The gate jobs were red because they installed only <code>.[dev]</code> while the UI tests need <code>wx</code> and the typing gate needs the optional modules ignored; the fix (install <code>.[dev,ui]</code> in the test jobs and add <code>markitdown</code>/<code>certifi</code>/<code>pdfplumber</code>/<code>pyttsx3</code> to the mypy overrides) is committed and verified — PR CI, Security CI, and Accessibility CI all conclude success on <code>main</code> (commit d62d3bd). OPS-1 is Done.</p> <p>The agreed next sequence (decided with the maintainer): (1) do the cheap Tier 4 quality cleanup first — CQ-18 (the ~43 remaining E501 line-length wraps, after which the <code>--ignore E501</code> can be removed from the lint gate and pre-commit) and individually verify/close the TYPE-1..8 rows — because it is low-risk, behavior-preserving, and makes every later PR land in a clean tree; (2) then build Tier 2 flagship work (QUILL key QK-1..5/QK-9, navigation NAV-1/4/5, selection SEL-1..3, then AI-13 provider wiring); (3) do the big <code>MainFrame</code> decomposition (CQ-1/CQ-16) last, behind the GATE-6 characterization snapshot, never before Tier 2. Tiers are then executed in order through Tier 6: Tier 3 GLOW (engine adopted via quill-glow-core, structured-format audit as the headline), Tier 4 structural health, Tier 5 BITS Whisperer transcription (the docs/BITS swap puts transcription before documentation), and Tier 6 documentation last.</p> <p>Parallelization guidance (no code collision): the cheap cleanup and the flagship work own disjoint directories, so they can run as separate agents — Agent A on <code>quill/io</code>, <code>quill/core</code> (non-<code>ai</code>), <code>quill/platform</code>, <code>quill/tools</code>; Agent B on the QUILL key and NAV/SEL features in <code>quill/ui/</code> plus new <code>quill/core/</code> modules; Agent C on docs under <code>docs/</code>. The single hard rule: only one agent may touch <code>quill/ui/main_frame.py</code> at a time, because it is the 18k-line shared surface, and the <code>MainFrame</code> decomposition must be serialized after the features land. Each agent regenerates <code>golden.html</code> with pandoc, updates this tracker, and commits per item.</p> <h4 id="2026-06-01-fixes--planning-two-reported-dialog-bugs-fixed-and-a-principled-dialog-standardization-decision">2026-06-01 (fixes + planning): two reported dialog bugs fixed, and a principled dialog-standardization decision</h4> <p>Two GitHub issues reported by a beta tester were fixed on <code>main</code> and a design question about the many hand-rolled dialogs was settled.</p> <ul> <li>#84 (could not exit the find-within-files dialog): <code>_prompt_file_search</code> added its <code>StdDialogButtonSizer</code> with <code>wx.ALIGN_RIGHT</code> instead of <code>wx.EXPAND</code>, so on Windows the OK/Cancel buttons were left unrealized and Cancel could not be clicked, trapping the user. The fix mirrors the proven single-document search dialog (<code>_prompt_search</code>): the button sizer is added with <code>wx.EXPAND</code>, an explicit default button is set, Enter submits, and focus is deferred via <code>CallAfter</code>. The identical bug in the sibling Status Bar Layout dialog (which also never gave the dialog its own outer sizer) was fixed in the same change, and the misspelling-review dialog got its missing outer sizer too.</li> <li>#85 (Go To and List Bookmarks did nothing): both bookmark dialogs were hardened. <code>go_to_bookmark</code> now forces a default selection and wires affirmative/escape ids so it is reliably dismissible; <code>_show_tree_navigator</code> (List Bookmarks) now wires modal ids, focuses the tree via <code>CallAfter</code>, and guarantees <code>Destroy()</code> in a <code>finally</code>. Regression tests cover the jump, the modal-id wiring, and the empty-bookmarks announcement.</li> <li>Dialog-standardization decision (principled, no damage): a wholesale rewrite was deliberately rejected. The evidence is that the bug class is inconsistent construction, not custom dialogs per se — the near-identical <code>_prompt_search</code> worked while <code>_prompt_file_search</code> did not. wxPython also has no stock multi-field form dialog, and the interactive AI-tool dialogs are working, accessible-today features that the maintainer's own assessment on issue #73 says must be converted individually with a screen reader, never batch-rewritten. So the plan adds a narrow, additive pair: <code>A11Y-4</code> is sharpened into a machine-enforced dialog-contract guard (a GATE-6-style conformance test for the exact <code>EXPAND</code>/outer-sizer/default-button/<code>Destroy</code> contract), <code>DLG-1</code> (Tier 2) migrates only the safe submit-once forms onto the already-shipped <code>show_web_form</code> helper, and <code>DLG-2</code> (Tier 4) defers the interactive tools to individual screen-reader-verified conversions. Backlog totals moved from 164 to 166; Tier 2 grew to 44 and Tier 4 to 30.</li> </ul> <h4 id="2026-06-01-later-still-tier-1-complete--full-gate-ladder-enforced-tree-gate-clean">2026-06-01 (later still): Tier 1 complete — full gate ladder enforced, tree gate-clean</h4> <p>What shipped and is committed to <code>main</code>, completing Tier 1:</p> <ul> <li>The remaining five gates are done. <code>pr-ci.yml</code> adds the required PR pipeline (GATE-2) with a lint job (format check plus <code>ruff check</code> with E501 deferred to CQ-18), a <code>unit-tests</code> job that runs the full suite under a hard 70% core+io coverage floor (GATE-5, current 72.28%), and a <code>characterization</code> job (GATE-6). <code>security-ci.yml</code> adds a <code>security-checks</code> job (GATE-8) running the hardened-XML check and the TLS, redaction, XML-bomb, zip-cap, sandbox, and OCR-allowlist invariant suites. Accessibility CI now also runs the announcement-grammar conformance suite (GATE-7).</li> <li>The characterization gate freezes the <code>MainFrame</code> public surface (235 methods) in a committed snapshot via <code>quill/tools/ui_surface.py</code>, so the planned decomposition stays behavior-preserving; the snapshot is regenerated deliberately with <code>python -m quill.tools.ui_surface --write</code>.</li> <li>The cheap quality cleanup that makes the gates honest landed with it: the whole first-party tree was formatted (<code>ruff format</code>, ~90 files) and the auto-fixable lint cleared (imports, bugbear <code>zip(strict=)</code>, pyupgrade, stray E402), leaving only the E501 line-length backlog deferred to CQ-18. A vendored/distribution <code>extend-exclude</code> keeps the gate scoped to real source.</li> <li>A real ordering bug was found and fixed in the process: the autosave uniqueness suffix <code>-001</code> sorted <em>before</em> the bare <code>.snap</code> name (because <code>-</code> < <code>.</code>), so <code>latest_autosave</code> could return the older snapshot when two saves shared a microsecond stamp. Every snapshot now carries a zero-padded counter so filenames always sort in write order; verified stable across repeated runs.</li> </ul> <p>Honest standing after this entry: Tier 1 is 20 of 20 — done. The safety floor, the typing zone, the accessibility grammar, and the no-silent-network promise are all machine-enforced on every PR, and the tree is clean enough that the next work lands safely. Still nothing user-facing and flagship has shipped (Tier 2 remains 0 of 29); per the agreed plan the next move is the cheap Tier 4 quality cleanup (CQ-18, TYPE rows) and then the flagship QUILL key and navigation work, with the monolith decomposition deliberately last.</p> <h4 id="2026-06-01-later-gate-ladder-enforced-in-ci-scoped-typing-clean-and-the-announcement-grammar-landed">2026-06-01 (later): Gate ladder enforced in CI, scoped typing clean, and the announcement grammar landed</h4> <p>What shipped and is committed to <code>main</code>, building directly on the morning's Tier 1 entry:</p> <ul> <li>The quality-gate ladder is now real in CI, not just described. A required Security CI pipeline runs three new jobs that block merge: <code>scoped-strict-typing</code> (<code>mypy quill\core quill\io</code>, GATE-3 and CQ-7), <code>banned-patterns</code> (GATE-4), and <code>no-silent-network</code> (GATE-9). Pre-commit hooks (GATE-1) catch format, lint, and undefined-name problems before a commit, and CONTRIBUTING documents the scoped type-check command and why the whole-tree scan is never used (PERF-8).</li> <li>The scoped strict-typing zone is genuinely clean: all 33 real type errors across <code>quill/core</code> and <code>quill/io</code> were fixed (missing annotations, <code>Any</code> returns, a wrong tuple annotation, typed file-lock handles, a type-safe snippet clone), and stub-less third-party imports are handled with explicit module overrides so the gate measures our own code. <code>mypy</code> reports zero errors across 96 source files.</li> <li>The banned-pattern gate is an AST checker, not a brittle text grep: it fails on bare <code>wx.</code> in <code>main_frame</code> where <code>wx</code> is not locally bound (the BUG-2 class), on raw <code>ET.fromstring</code> outside <code>safe_xml</code> (the SEC-10 class), and on any weakening of ruff's undefined-name and redefinition rules (the BUG-1 and BUG-4 classes), with tests proving it catches each shape.</li> <li>The no-silent-network gate inventories every outbound call site in the package and fails when a new one appears without a reviewed rationale tying it to a user action or explicit consent. All eight current egress points are documented.</li> <li>Two more cheap protections landed: every HTTPS call now goes through a single verified TLS context (SEC-5), and OCR language codes are validated against a strict allowlist before Tesseract is invoked so a tampered setting cannot inject CLI options (SEC-3).</li> <li>Accessibility gained a shared announcement grammar: a written style guide plus a UI-agnostic <code>quill/core/announcements.py</code> (<code>format_announcement</code>, <code>format_progress</code>, <code>pluralize</code>) that the AI writing-action announcer now uses, with tests verifying the key phrases (A11Y-1).</li> </ul> <p>Honest standing after this entry: Tier 1 is 15 of 20 items done; the five remaining are gate work that needs more infrastructure (the full PR pipeline GATE-2, coverage floor GATE-5, the characterization suite GATE-6, the accessibility-PR gate GATE-7, and the security-scan gate GATE-8). The accessibility-PR gate (GATE-7) now has a concrete grammar to check against, which de-risks it. Nothing user-facing and flagship has started yet (Tier 2 remains at 0 of 29). The safety floor and the team's velocity protections are now materially higher; the next strategic move is the flagship QUILL key and navigation work, not more gates.</p> <h4 id="2026-06-01-tier-1-protections-landed-latent-crash-bugs-cheap-security-hardening-ai-error-taxonomy">2026-06-01: Tier 1 protections landed (latent-crash bugs, cheap security hardening, AI error taxonomy)</h4> <p>What shipped and is committed to <code>main</code>:</p> <ul> <li>All seven verified latent-crash bugs are fixed with regression tests: the corrupted onboarding method and its three deleted helpers (BUG-1), the four bare <code>wx.</code> heading-style calls (BUG-2), the undefined <code>VoiceOption</code> annotations (BUG-3), the duplicate <code>URLError</code> import (BUG-4), the unchecked llama.cpp response shape (BUG-5), the DOCX or PPTX element name collision (BUG-6), and the non-positive chunk-split guard (BUG-7).</li> <li>The cheap, user-protecting security hardening is in: all untrusted XML now parses with entity expansion disabled against billion-laughs (SEC-10), ZIP-based formats enforce a cumulative decompression cap (SEC-11), diagnostics redaction covers token, password, and NAME_KEY patterns (SEC-13), and configured speech executable paths are validated against an allowlist so tampered settings cannot launch arbitrary programs (SEC-1).</li> <li>Two pre-existing test failures, unrelated to the above but found along the way, were fixed honestly rather than ignored: an autosave snapshot-collision bug where a coarse Windows clock overwrote snapshots, and four stale keymap tests that had not been updated after the deliberate QUILL key chord migration.</li> <li>The in-progress AI connection work was reviewed, judged safe and forward-aligned, and committed: a structured error taxonomy (auth 401, forbidden 403, rate-limited, warming-up, not-running, timeout, unreachable) matched on numeric status codes rather than substrings, a bounded warm-up retry, and portable-key unlock-failure recovery. This is the foundation the AI-13 provider wiring and AI-17 chat-path messages build on.</li> </ul> <p>Honest dimension movement since the 0.1.5 baseline:</p> <table> <thead> <tr> <th>Dimension</th> <th>Baseline (0.1.5)</th> <th>Now (2026-06-01)</th> <th>Why it moved</th> </tr> </thead> <tbody> <tr> <td>Security and privacy</td> <td>Level 3</td> <td>Level 3 to 4</td> <td>Untrusted-input hardening (XML, zip, redaction) and executable-path validation close the worst input and supply-chain gaps; remaining items (network checksums, sandbox limits, gate enforcement) keep this short of Level 5.</td> </tr> <tr> <td>Code quality and architecture</td> <td>Level 3</td> <td>Level 3 (cleaner)</td> <td>Seven latent crashes removed and pre-existing test rot fixed; the UI monolith and lint/typing debt remain, so the level holds.</td> </tr> <tr> <td>AI and agents</td> <td>Level 2</td> <td>Level 2 to 3</td> <td>Connection diagnostics are now honest and screen-reader-friendly; the headline gap (configured providers still fall back to local generation, AI-13) is not yet closed.</td> </tr> <tr> <td>Test coverage</td> <td>Level 2 to 3</td> <td>Level 3</td> <td>New regression tests for every bug class and the AI taxonomy; the broad UI characterization and IO matrix work is still ahead.</td> </tr> </tbody> </table> <p>What is explicitly not done yet: the quality-gate ladder is not enforced in CI (GATE items), the configured AI providers still do not drive generation (AI-13), the UI monolith is intact (CQ-1), and the flagship QUILL key, navigation, selection, and settings work (Tier 2) has not started. Overall standing remains roughly Level 3, already Level 4 on accessibility; this section moved the safety floor up without yet adding user-facing flagship value.</p> <h3 id="what-quill-would-be">What QUILL would be</h3> <p>QUILL would be a mature, 1.0-grade, screen-reader-first writing application: not a promising beta, but a dependable daily tool that a blind or low-vision writer could trust for real work, and that a sighted power user would also find fast and pleasant. The signature QUILL key would be a genuinely memorable interaction (discoverable with one question mark, tunable, consistent, and documented as the headline feature), and the accessibility agent would be a category-defining capability: tell the app to make a document accessible, and watch it do so, step by step, reversibly, entirely by keyboard and voice. The AI surface would also be honest end to end: when a user picks OpenAI, OpenRouter, Gemini, Azure, Ollama, or Claude, that provider is the one that responds, instead of the configuration being accepted while generation silently falls back to the local model (AI-13). GLOW would be folded in as the in-app accessibility engine, and transcription would arrive last as a unifying capability, in that deliberate order.</p> <h3 id="scale-on-the-dimensions-that-matter">Scale on the dimensions that matter</h3> <p>The following self-assessment uses a simple five-level scale: Level 1 prototype, Level 2 beta, Level 3 product, Level 4 polished product, Level 5 best-in-class or category-defining. Today's honest baseline is included for contrast.</p> <table> <thead> <tr> <th>Dimension</th> <th>Today (0.1.5 beta)</th> <th>After this plan</th> <th>Notes</th> </tr> </thead> <tbody> <tr> <td>Accessibility</td> <td>Level 4: strong, screen-reader-first</td> <td>Level 5: category-defining</td> <td>Accessibility is gated on every change; the accessibility agent has no real peer.</td> </tr> <tr> <td>Core writing and navigation</td> <td>Level 3 to 4</td> <td>Level 5</td> <td>QUILL key, Quick Nav, and structural selection become a cohesive, discoverable, tunable system.</td> </tr> <tr> <td>AI and agents</td> <td>Level 2: strong config, but providers do not yet generate</td> <td>Level 4 to 5</td> <td>Today the provider boundary, key storage, and model discovery are excellent, but a configured cloud or Ollama provider silently falls back to the local model (AI-13); after this plan, local-first, consented, reversible, fully accessible AI editing and a real accessibility agent, with the selected provider actually responding.</td> </tr> <tr> <td>Code quality and architecture</td> <td>Level 3: well-structured but a monolith</td> <td>Level 5</td> <td>The UI monolith is fully decomposed behind characterization tests; strict typing and lint are clean and enforced everywhere, not only in the core zone.</td> </tr> <tr> <td>Security and privacy</td> <td>Level 3</td> <td>Level 5</td> <td>Untrusted-input hardening, supply-chain checks, machine-enforced no-silent-network, and a documented plugin capability and signing model (ECO-1).</td> </tr> <tr> <td>Performance</td> <td>Level 3</td> <td>Level 5</td> <td>First-use stalls removed; budgets enforced by a gate; large-document and Read Aloud paths tuned to best-in-class responsiveness.</td> </tr> <tr> <td>Documentation</td> <td>Level 3 to 4</td> <td>Level 5</td> <td>Consolidated, accessible, complete, with primers, references, podcasts, tutorials, and full localization (L10N-1).</td> </tr> <tr> <td>Combined suite (GLOW and transcription)</td> <td>Level 2: separate apps, partial bridges</td> <td>Level 5</td> <td>Write, transcribe, audit, fix, and publish in one accessible app on shared engines.</td> </tr> <tr> <td>Engineering process and gates</td> <td>Level 2: tools exist, not enforced</td> <td>Level 5</td> <td>A full gate ladder makes quality objective and lets AI assistance accelerate safely.</td> </tr> <tr> <td>Test coverage</td> <td>Level 2 to 3</td> <td>Level 5</td> <td>UI characterization, IO matrix, sandbox policy, per-provider contract tests, and a performance budget close every major gap, with a ratcheting coverage floor.</td> </tr> <tr> <td>Platform breadth</td> <td>Level 3: Windows and macOS</td> <td>Level 5</td> <td>Windows and macOS are excellent and Linux reaches the same accessibility and stability bar (LINUX-2).</td> </tr> </tbody> </table> <h3 id="overall-standing">Overall standing</h3> <ul> <li>Today: a strong, accessibility-leading 0.1.5 beta with an excellent foundation, a few latent bugs, a large UI monolith, an AI provider surface whose configuration outruns its wiring (cloud and Ollama selections do not yet drive generation), and quality tools that are present but not yet enforced. Roughly Level 3 overall, already Level 4 on accessibility.</li> <li>After this plan: a confident, releasable product at Level 5 across every dimension that matters, and clearly category-defining on accessibility and on accessible AI-assisted editing. It would be, credibly, the most accessible writing application available, with an agent capability that no mainstream editor offers, backed by an engineering process strong enough to keep it that way. Because the plan now commits to closing every dimension to highest quality (the former frontiers below are committed work, not deferred hopes), nothing of substance is left at less than best-in-class once it is complete.</li> </ul> <h3 id="closing-every-remaining-gap-to-highest-quality">Closing every remaining gap to highest quality</h3> <p>The earlier draft of this plan listed frontiers that would remain even after full implementation. To meet the directive that nothing be left below highest quality, those frontiers are now committed plan items rather than acknowledged shortfalls:</p> <ul> <li>Platform breadth. Beyond the Linux spike (LINUX-1), the plan now commits to an accessible Linux platform layer at the same bar as Windows and macOS (LINUX-2).</li> <li>Collaboration. Real-time multi-user editing stays deliberately out of scope (with the reason recorded), but the plan commits to local-first, accessible asynchronous review and sharing so collaboration is not a blank (COLLAB-1).</li> <li>Ecosystem. The plan commits to a documented plugin capability and signing model and a review process, so a safe third-party ecosystem can grow off the experimental flag (ECO-1, building on SEC-8).</li> <li>Localization depth. The plan commits to full UI and core-documentation localization for the launch languages, with a process to keep translations current (L10N-1).</li> </ul> <p>These are large (XL) investments and are sequenced into Tier 6 after the 1.0 wins, but they are now part of the committed plan, so the honest answer to "what would still fall short?" is: nothing of substance. The only remaining limits are deliberate scope choices (no real-time co-editing) and the permanent reality that a living product keeps improving with user feedback.</p> </body> </html>
